<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_education">Early life and education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_VII&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life and education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_VII_(1841_%E2%80%93_1910).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Edward_VII_%281841_%E2%80%93_1910%29.jpg/170px-Edward_VII_%281841_%E2%80%93_1910%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="248" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Edward_VII_%281841_%E2%80%93_1910%29.jpg/255px-Edward_VII_%281841_%E2%80%93_1910%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Edward_VII_%281841_%E2%80%93_1910%29.jpg/340px-Edward_VII_%281841_%E2%80%93_1910%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1027" data-file-height="1500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_VII_(1841_%E2%80%93_1910).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of Albert Edward, Prince of Wales, by <a href="/wiki/Franz_Xaver_Winterhalter" title="Franz Xaver Winterhalter">Winterhalter</a>, 1846</div></div></div>
<p>Edward was born at 10:48 in the morning on 9 November 1841 in <a href="/wiki/Buckingham_Palace" title="Buckingham Palace">Buckingham Palace</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> He was the eldest son and second child of <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a> and her husband <a href="/wiki/Prince_Albert_of_Saxe-Coburg_and_Gotha" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince Albert of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha">Prince Albert of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha</a>. He was christened <i>Albert Edward</i> at <a href="/wiki/St_George%27s_Chapel,_Windsor_Castle" title="St George&#39;s Chapel, Windsor Castle">St George's Chapel, Windsor Castle</a>, on 25 January 1842.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> He was named Albert after his father and Edward after his maternal grandfather <a href="/wiki/Prince_Edward,_Duke_of_Kent_and_Strathearn" title="Prince Edward, Duke of Kent and Strathearn">Prince Edward, Duke of Kent and Strathearn</a>. He was known as <i>Bertie</i> to the royal family throughout his life.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As the eldest son of the British sovereign, he was automatically <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Cornwall" title="Duke of Cornwall">Duke of Cornwall</a> and <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Rothesay" title="Duke of Rothesay">Duke of Rothesay</a> at birth. As a son of Prince Albert, he also held the titles of <a href="/wiki/Prince_of_Saxe-Coburg_and_Gotha" title="Prince of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha">Prince of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ernestine_duchies" title="Ernestine duchies">Duke of Saxony</a>. He was created <a href="/wiki/Prince_of_Wales" title="Prince of Wales">Prince of Wales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Chester" title="Earl of Chester">Earl of Chester</a> on 8 December 1841, <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Dublin" title="Earl of Dublin">Earl of Dublin</a> on 10 September 1849<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> or 17 January 1850,<sup id="cite_ref-weir_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weir-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cp_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cp-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Knight_of_the_Garter" class="mw-redirect" title="Knight of the Garter">Knight of the Garter</a> on 9 November 1858, and a <a href="/wiki/Knight_of_the_Thistle" class="mw-redirect" title="Knight of the Thistle">Knight of the Thistle</a> on 24 May 1867.<sup id="cite_ref-weir_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weir-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> In 1863, he renounced his succession rights to the Duchy of <a href="/wiki/Saxe-Coburg_and_Gotha" title="Saxe-Coburg and Gotha">Saxe-Coburg and Gotha</a> in favour of his younger brother, <a href="/wiki/Alfred,_Duke_of_Saxe-Coburg_and_Gotha" title="Alfred, Duke of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha">Prince Alfred</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Queen and Prince Albert were determined that their eldest son should have an education that would prepare him to be a model <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarch" class="mw-redirect" title="Constitutional monarch">constitutional monarch</a>. At age seven, Edward embarked on a rigorous educational programme devised by Albert, and supervised by several tutors. Unlike his elder sister <a href="/wiki/Victoria,_Princess_Royal" title="Victoria, Princess Royal">Victoria</a>, he did not excel in his studies.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> He tried to meet the expectations of his parents, but to no avail. Although Edward was not a diligent student—his true talents were those of charm, sociability and tact—<a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli" title="Benjamin Disraeli">Benjamin Disraeli</a> described him as informed, intelligent and of sweet manner.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> After the completion of his secondary-level studies, his tutor was replaced by a personal governor, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bruce_(1813%E2%80%931862)" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Bruce (1813–1862)">Robert Bruce</a>.
</p><p>After an educational trip to Rome, undertaken in the first few months of 1859, Edward spent the summer of that year studying at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh">University of Edinburgh</a> under, among others, the chemist <a href="/wiki/Lyon_Playfair" class="mw-redirect" title="Lyon Playfair">Lyon Playfair</a>. In October, he matriculated as an undergraduate at <a href="/wiki/Christ_Church,_Oxford" title="Christ Church, Oxford">Christ Church, Oxford</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Now released from the educational strictures imposed by his parents, he enjoyed studying for the first time and performed satisfactorily in examinations.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> In 1861, he transferred to <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College,_Cambridge" title="Trinity College, Cambridge">Trinity College, Cambridge</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> where he was tutored in history by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Kingsley" title="Charles Kingsley">Charles Kingsley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Regius_Professor_of_Modern_History_(Cambridge)" class="mw-redirect" title="Regius Professor of Modern History (Cambridge)">Regius Professor of Modern History</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Kingsley's efforts brought forth the best academic performances of Edward's life, and Edward actually looked forward to his lectures.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_adulthood">Early adulthood</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_VII&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early adulthood">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:H.R.H._Prince_of_Wales_and_Suite_at_Point_View,_Niagara.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/H.R.H._Prince_of_Wales_and_Suite_at_Point_View%2C_Niagara.jpg/220px-H.R.H._Prince_of_Wales_and_Suite_at_Point_View%2C_Niagara.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="282" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/H.R.H._Prince_of_Wales_and_Suite_at_Point_View%2C_Niagara.jpg/330px-H.R.H._Prince_of_Wales_and_Suite_at_Point_View%2C_Niagara.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/H.R.H._Prince_of_Wales_and_Suite_at_Point_View%2C_Niagara.jpg/440px-H.R.H._Prince_of_Wales_and_Suite_at_Point_View%2C_Niagara.jpg 2x" data-file-width="453" data-file-height="580" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:H.R.H._Prince_of_Wales_and_Suite_at_Point_View,_Niagara.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Edward at <a href="/wiki/Niagara_Falls" title="Niagara Falls">Niagara Falls</a>, 1860</div></div></div>
<p>In 1860, Edward undertook the first tour of North America by a Prince of Wales. His genial good humour and confident <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/bonhomie" class="extiw" title="wiktionary:bonhomie">bonhomie</a></i> made the tour a great success.<sup id="cite_ref-bc_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bc-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> He inaugurated the <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Bridge,_Montreal" class="mw-redirect" title="Victoria Bridge, Montreal">Victoria Bridge, Montreal</a>, across the <a href="/wiki/St_Lawrence_River" class="mw-redirect" title="St Lawrence River">St Lawrence River</a>, and laid the cornerstone of <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Hill,_Ottawa" class="mw-redirect" title="Parliament Hill, Ottawa">Parliament Hill, Ottawa</a>. He watched <a href="/wiki/Charles_Blondin" title="Charles Blondin">Charles Blondin</a> traverse <a href="/wiki/Niagara_Falls" title="Niagara Falls">Niagara Falls</a> by highwire, and stayed for three days with President <a href="/wiki/James_Buchanan" title="James Buchanan">James Buchanan</a> at the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>. Buchanan accompanied the Prince to <a href="/wiki/Mount_Vernon" title="Mount Vernon">Mount Vernon</a>, to pay his respects at the tomb of <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>. Vast crowds greeted him everywhere. He met <a href="/wiki/Henry_Wadsworth_Longfellow" title="Henry Wadsworth Longfellow">Henry Wadsworth Longfellow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Waldo_Emerson" title="Ralph Waldo Emerson">Ralph Waldo Emerson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Wendell_Holmes,_Sr." class="mw-redirect" title="Oliver Wendell Holmes, Sr.">Oliver Wendell Holmes, Sr.</a> Prayers for the royal family were said in <a href="/wiki/Trinity_Church,_New_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Trinity Church, New York">Trinity Church, New York</a>, for the first time since 1776.<sup id="cite_ref-bc_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bc-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The four-month tour throughout Canada and the United States considerably boosted Edward's confidence and self-esteem, and had many diplomatic benefits for Great Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Edward had hoped to pursue a career in the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a>, but his mother vetoed an active military career.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> He had been gazetted colonel on 9 November 1858<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>—to his disappointment, as he had wanted to earn his commission by examination.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> In September 1861, Edward was sent to Germany, supposedly to watch military manoeuvres, but actually in order to engineer a meeting between him and Princess <a href="/wiki/Alexandra_of_Denmark" title="Alexandra of Denmark">Alexandra of Denmark</a>, the eldest daughter of <a href="/wiki/Christian_IX_of_Denmark" title="Christian IX of Denmark">Prince Christian of Denmark</a> and his wife <a href="/wiki/Louise_of_Hesse-Kassel" title="Louise of Hesse-Kassel">Louise</a>. The Queen and Prince Albert had already decided that Edward and Alexandra should marry. They met at <a href="/wiki/Speyer" title="Speyer">Speyer</a> on 24 September under the auspices of his elder sister, Victoria, who had married the <a href="/wiki/Frederick_III,_German_Emperor" title="Frederick III, German Emperor">Crown Prince of Prussia</a> in 1858.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Edward's sister, acting upon instructions from their mother, had met Alexandra at <a href="/wiki/Neustrelitz" title="Neustrelitz">Strelitz</a> in June; the young Danish princess made a very favourable impression. Edward and Alexandra were friendly from the start; the meeting went well for both sides, and marriage plans advanced.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Edward gained a reputation as a playboy. Determined to get some army experience, he attended manoeuvres in Ireland, during which he spent three nights with an actress, <a href="/wiki/Nellie_Clifden" title="Nellie Clifden">Nellie Clifden</a>, who was hidden in the camp by his fellow officers.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Prince Albert, though ill, was appalled and visited Edward at <a href="/wiki/Cambridge" title="Cambridge">Cambridge</a> to issue a reprimand. Albert died in December 1861 just two weeks after the visit. Queen Victoria was inconsolable, wore mourning clothes for the rest of her life and blamed Edward for his father's death.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> At first, she regarded her son with distaste as frivolous, indiscreet and irresponsible. She wrote to her eldest daughter, "I never can, or shall, look at him without a shudder."<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage">Marriage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_VII&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Marriage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Once widowed, Queen Victoria effectively withdrew from public life. Shortly after Prince Albert's death, she arranged for Edward to embark on an extensive tour of the Middle East, visiting <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>, <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beirut" title="Beirut">Beirut</a> and <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Constantinople</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/British_Government" class="mw-redirect" title="British Government">British Government</a> wanted Edward to secure the friendship of Egypt's ruler, <a href="/wiki/Sa%27id_of_Egypt" title="Sa&#39;id of Egypt">Said Pasha</a>, to prevent French control of the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> if the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> collapsed. It was the first <a href="/wiki/Royal_tour" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal tour">royal tour</a> on which an official photographer, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bedford_(photographer)" title="Francis Bedford (photographer)">Francis Bedford</a>, was in attendance. As soon as Edward returned to Britain, preparations were made for his engagement, which was sealed at <a href="/wiki/Laeken" title="Laeken">Laeken</a> in Belgium on 9 September 1862.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Edward married Alexandra of Denmark at <a href="/wiki/St_George%27s_Chapel,_Windsor_Castle" title="St George&#39;s Chapel, Windsor Castle">St George's Chapel, Windsor Castle</a>, on 10 March 1863. He was 21; she was 18.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wedding_of_Albert_Edward_Prince_of_Wales_and_Alexandra_of_Denmark_1863.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Wedding_of_Albert_Edward_Prince_of_Wales_and_Alexandra_of_Denmark_1863.jpg/220px-Wedding_of_Albert_Edward_Prince_of_Wales_and_Alexandra_of_Denmark_1863.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="290" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Wedding_of_Albert_Edward_Prince_of_Wales_and_Alexandra_of_Denmark_1863.jpg/330px-Wedding_of_Albert_Edward_Prince_of_Wales_and_Alexandra_of_Denmark_1863.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Wedding_of_Albert_Edward_Prince_of_Wales_and_Alexandra_of_Denmark_1863.jpg/440px-Wedding_of_Albert_Edward_Prince_of_Wales_and_Alexandra_of_Denmark_1863.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1143" data-file-height="1508" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wedding_of_Albert_Edward_Prince_of_Wales_and_Alexandra_of_Denmark_1863.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Edward and Alexandra on their wedding day, 1863</div></div></div>
<p>The couple established <a href="/wiki/Marlborough_House" title="Marlborough House">Marlborough House</a> as their London residence and <a href="/wiki/Sandringham_House" title="Sandringham House">Sandringham House</a> in Norfolk as their country retreat. They entertained on a lavish scale. Their marriage met with disapproval in certain circles because most of Queen Victoria's relations were German, and Denmark was at loggerheads with Germany over the territories of <a href="/wiki/Schleswig" class="mw-redirect" title="Schleswig">Schleswig</a> and <a href="/wiki/Holstein" title="Holstein">Holstein</a>. When Alexandra's father inherited the throne of Denmark in November 1863, the <a href="/wiki/German_Confederation" title="German Confederation">German Confederation</a> took the opportunity to <a href="/wiki/Second_Schleswig_War" title="Second Schleswig War">invade and annex Schleswig-Holstein</a>. The Queen was of two minds as to whether it was a suitable match, given the political climate.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> After the marriage, she expressed anxiety about their socialite lifestyle and attempted to dictate to them on various matters, including the names of their children.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Edward had mistresses throughout his married life. He socialised with actress <a href="/wiki/Lillie_Langtry" title="Lillie Langtry">Lillie Langtry</a>; <a href="/wiki/Lady_Randolph_Churchill" title="Lady Randolph Churchill">Lady Randolph Churchill</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Daisy_Greville,_Countess_of_Warwick" title="Daisy Greville, Countess of Warwick">Daisy Greville, Countess of Warwick</a>; actress <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Bernhardt" title="Sarah Bernhardt">Sarah Bernhardt</a>; noblewoman <a href="/wiki/Lady_Susan_Vane-Tempest" title="Lady Susan Vane-Tempest">Lady Susan Vane-Tempest</a>; singer <a href="/wiki/Hortense_Schneider" title="Hortense Schneider">Hortense Schneider</a>; prostitute Giulia Beneni (known as "La Barucci"); wealthy humanitarian <a href="/wiki/Agnes_Keyser" title="Agnes Keyser">Agnes Keyser</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Alice_Keppel" title="Alice Keppel">Alice Keppel</a>. At least fifty-five liaisons are conjectured.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> How far these relationships went is not always clear. Edward always strove to be discreet, but this did not prevent society gossip or press speculation.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Keppel's great-granddaughter, <a href="/wiki/Camilla_Parker_Bowles" class="mw-redirect" title="Camilla Parker Bowles">Camilla Parker Bowles</a>, became the mistress and subsequent wife of <a href="/wiki/Charles,_Prince_of_Wales" title="Charles, Prince of Wales">Charles, Prince of Wales</a>, Edward's great-great-grandson. It was rumoured that Camilla's grandmother, Sonia Keppel, was fathered by Edward, but she was "almost certainly" the daughter of <a href="/wiki/George_Keppel_(British_Army_officer,_born_1865)" title="George Keppel (British Army officer, born 1865)">George Keppel</a>, whom she resembled.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Edward never acknowledged any illegitimate children.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Alexandra was aware of his affairs, and seems to have accepted them.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1869, <a href="/wiki/Sir_Charles_Mordaunt,_10th_Baronet" title="Sir Charles Mordaunt, 10th Baronet">Sir Charles Mordaunt</a>, a <a href="/wiki/British_Member_of_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="British Member of Parliament">British Member of Parliament</a>, threatened to name Edward as co-respondent in his divorce suit. Ultimately, he did not do so but Edward was called as a witness in the case in early 1870. It was shown that Edward had visited the Mordaunts' house while Sir Charles was away sitting in the <a href="/wiki/British_House_of_Commons" class="mw-redirect" title="British House of Commons">House of Commons</a>. Although nothing further was proven and Edward denied he had committed adultery, the suggestion of impropriety was damaging.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Heir_apparent">Heir apparent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_VII&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Heir apparent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>During Queen Victoria's widowhood, Edward pioneered the idea of royal public appearances as they are understood today—for example, opening the <a href="/wiki/Thames_Embankment" title="Thames Embankment">Thames Embankment</a> in 1871, the <a href="/wiki/Mersey_Railway" title="Mersey Railway">Mersey Tunnel</a> in 1886, and <a href="/wiki/Tower_Bridge" title="Tower Bridge">Tower Bridge</a> in 1894<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>—but his mother did not allow him an active role in the running of the country until 1898.<sup id="cite_ref-royal_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-royal-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> He was sent summaries of important government documents, but she refused to give him access to the originals.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Edward annoyed his mother, who favoured the Germans, by siding with Denmark on the <a href="/wiki/Schleswig-Holstein_Question" title="Schleswig-Holstein Question">Schleswig-Holstein Question</a> in 1864 and in the same year annoyed her again by making a special effort to meet <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Garibaldi" title="Giuseppe Garibaldi">Giuseppe Garibaldi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal</a> Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Ewart Gladstone</a> sent him papers secretly.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> From 1886, <a href="/wiki/Foreign_Secretary" class="mw-redirect" title="Foreign Secretary">Foreign Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/Lord_Rosebery" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Rosebery">Lord Rosebery</a> sent him Foreign Office despatches, and from 1892 some <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Cabinet of the United Kingdom">Cabinet</a> papers were opened to him.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1870 republican sentiment in Britain was given a boost when the French emperor, <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_III" title="Napoleon III">Napoleon III</a>, was defeated in the <a href="/wiki/Franco-Prussian_War" title="Franco-Prussian War">Franco-Prussian War</a> and the <a href="/wiki/French_Third_Republic" title="French Third Republic">French Third Republic</a> was declared.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> However, in the winter of 1871, a brush with death led to an improvement in both Edward's popularity with the public and his relationship with his mother. While staying at Londesborough Lodge, near <a href="/wiki/Scarborough,_North_Yorkshire" title="Scarborough, North Yorkshire">Scarborough, North Yorkshire</a>, Edward contracted <a href="/wiki/Typhoid_fever" title="Typhoid fever">typhoid fever</a>, the disease that was believed to have killed his father. There was great national concern, and one of his fellow guests (<a href="/wiki/George_Stanhope,_7th_Earl_of_Chesterfield" title="George Stanhope, 7th Earl of Chesterfield">Lord Chesterfield</a>) died. Edward's recovery was greeted with almost universal relief.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Public celebrations included the composition of <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Sullivan" title="Arthur Sullivan">Arthur Sullivan</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Festival_Te_Deum" title="Festival Te Deum">Festival Te Deum</a>. Edward cultivated politicians from all parties, including republicans, as his friends, and thereby largely dissipated any residual feelings against him.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward,_Prince_of_Wales,_with_elephant,_Terai_cph.3b08927.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Edward%2C_Prince_of_Wales%2C_with_elephant%2C_Terai_cph.3b08927.jpg/220px-Edward%2C_Prince_of_Wales%2C_with_elephant%2C_Terai_cph.3b08927.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="291" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Edward%2C_Prince_of_Wales%2C_with_elephant%2C_Terai_cph.3b08927.jpg/330px-Edward%2C_Prince_of_Wales%2C_with_elephant%2C_Terai_cph.3b08927.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Edward%2C_Prince_of_Wales%2C_with_elephant%2C_Terai_cph.3b08927.jpg/440px-Edward%2C_Prince_of_Wales%2C_with_elephant%2C_Terai_cph.3b08927.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1131" data-file-height="1494" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward,_Prince_of_Wales,_with_elephant,_Terai_cph.3b08927.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Edward (front centre) in India, 1876</div></div></div>
<p>On 26 September 1875, Edward set off for India on an extensive eight-month tour; on the way, he visited Malta, <a href="/wiki/Brindisi" title="Brindisi">Brindisi</a> and Greece. His advisors remarked on his habit of treating all people the same, regardless of their social station or colour. In letters home, he complained of the treatment of the native Indians by the British officials: "Because a man has a black face and a different religion from our own, there is no reason why he should be treated as a brute."<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Consequently, <a href="/wiki/Lord_Salisbury" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Salisbury">Lord Salisbury</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_India" title="Secretary of State for India">Secretary of State for India</a>, issued new guidance and at least one <a href="/wiki/Resident_(title)" title="Resident (title)">resident</a> was removed from office.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> He returned to England on 11 May 1876, after stopping off at Portugal.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> At the end of the tour, Queen Victoria was given the title Empress of India by Parliament, in part as a result of the tour's success.<sup id="cite_ref-bc104_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bc104-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Edward was regarded worldwide as an arbiter of men's fashions.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> He made wearing <a href="/wiki/Tweed" title="Tweed">tweed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Homburg_hat" title="Homburg hat">Homburg hats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norfolk_jacket" title="Norfolk jacket">Norfolk jackets</a> fashionable, and popularised the wearing of black ties with dinner jackets, instead of <a href="/wiki/White_tie" title="White tie">white tie</a> and tails.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> He pioneered the pressing of trouser legs from side to side in preference to the now normal front and back creases,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> and was thought to have introduced the stand-up turn-down shirt collar, created for him by <a href="/wiki/Charvet_Place_Vend%C3%B4me#International_recognition" title="Charvet Place Vendôme">Charvet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> A stickler for proper dress, he is said to have admonished Lord Salisbury for wearing the trousers of an Elder Brother of <a href="/wiki/Trinity_House" title="Trinity House">Trinity House</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Privy Council">Privy Councillor</a>'s coat. Deep in an international crisis, Salisbury informed the Prince that it had been a dark morning, and that "my mind must have been occupied by some subject of less importance."<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The tradition of men not buttoning the bottom button of waistcoats is said to be linked to Edward, who supposedly left his undone because of his large girth.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> His waist measured 48&#160;inches (122&#160;cm) shortly before his coronation.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> He introduced the practice of eating roast beef and potatoes with horseradish sauce and <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire_pudding" title="Yorkshire pudding">yorkshire pudding</a> on Sundays, a meal that remains a staple British favourite for <a href="/wiki/Sunday_lunch" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunday lunch">Sunday lunch</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> He was not a heavy drinker, though he did drink champagne and, occasionally, port.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Edward was a patron of the arts and sciences and helped found the <a href="/wiki/Royal_College_of_Music" title="Royal College of Music">Royal College of Music</a>. He opened the college in 1883 with the words, "Class can no longer stand apart from class&#160;... I claim for music that it produces that union of feeling which I much desire to promote."<sup id="cite_ref-bc104_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bc104-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> At the same time, he enjoyed gambling and country sports and was an enthusiastic hunter. He ordered all the clocks at Sandringham to run half an hour ahead to provide more daylight time for shooting. This so-called tradition of <a href="/wiki/Sandringham_Time" class="mw-redirect" title="Sandringham Time">Sandringham Time</a> continued until 1936, when it was abolished by <a href="/wiki/Edward_VIII" title="Edward VIII">Edward VIII</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-duke_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-duke-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> He also laid out a golf course at Windsor. By the 1870s the future king had taken a keen interest in horseracing and steeplechasing. In 1896, his horse <a href="/wiki/Persimmon_(horse)" title="Persimmon (horse)">Persimmon</a> won both the <a href="/wiki/Derby_Stakes" class="mw-redirect" title="Derby Stakes">Derby Stakes</a> and the <a href="/wiki/St_Leger_Stakes" title="St Leger Stakes">St Leger Stakes</a>. In 1900, Persimmon's brother, <a href="/wiki/Diamond_Jubilee_(horse)" title="Diamond Jubilee (horse)">Diamond Jubilee</a>, won five races (Derby, St Leger, <a href="/wiki/2,000_Guineas_Stakes" class="mw-redirect" title="2,000 Guineas Stakes">2,000 Guineas Stakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Newmarket_Stakes" title="Newmarket Stakes">Newmarket Stakes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eclipse_Stakes" title="Eclipse Stakes">Eclipse Stakes</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> and another of Edward's horses, Ambush II, won the <a href="/wiki/Grand_National" title="Grand National">Grand National</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_family_group_1896.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ac/Royal_family_group_1896.jpg/220px-Royal_family_group_1896.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="290" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ac/Royal_family_group_1896.jpg/330px-Royal_family_group_1896.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ac/Royal_family_group_1896.jpg/440px-Royal_family_group_1896.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2497" data-file-height="3291" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_family_group_1896.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Edward (right) with his mother (centre) and Russian relations: <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_II_of_Russia" title="Nicholas II of Russia">Tsar Nicholas II</a> (left), <a href="/wiki/Alexandra_Feodorovna_(Alix_of_Hesse)" title="Alexandra Feodorovna (Alix of Hesse)">Empress Alexandra</a> and baby <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchess_Olga_Nikolaevna_of_Russia" title="Grand Duchess Olga Nikolaevna of Russia">Grand Duchess Olga Nikolaevna</a>, 1896</div></div></div>
<p>In 1891 Edward was embroiled in the <a href="/wiki/Royal_baccarat_scandal" title="Royal baccarat scandal">royal baccarat scandal</a>, when it was revealed he had played an illegal card game for money the previous year. The Prince was forced to appear as a witness in court for a second time when one of the participants unsuccessfully sued his fellow players for slander after being accused of cheating.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> In the same year Edward was involved in a personal conflict, when <a href="/wiki/Lord_Charles_Beresford" title="Lord Charles Beresford">Lord Charles Beresford</a> threatened to reveal details of Edward's private life to the press, as a protest against Edward interfering with Beresford's affair with <a href="/wiki/Daisy_Greville,_Countess_of_Warwick" title="Daisy Greville, Countess of Warwick">Daisy Greville, Countess of Warwick</a>. The friendship between the two men was irreversibly damaged, and their bitterness would last for the remainder of their lives.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Usually, Edward's outbursts of temper were short-lived, and "after he had let himself go&#160;... [he would] smooth matters by being especially nice".<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In late 1891, Edward's eldest son, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Victor" class="mw-redirect" title="Albert Victor">Albert Victor</a>, was engaged to <a href="/wiki/Princess_Victoria_Mary_of_Teck" class="mw-redirect" title="Princess Victoria Mary of Teck">Princess Victoria Mary of Teck</a>. Just a few weeks later, in early 1892, Albert Victor died of pneumonia. Edward was grief-stricken. "To lose our eldest son", he wrote, "is one of those calamities one can never really get over". Edward told Queen Victoria, "[I would] have given my life for him, as I put no value on mine".<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Albert Victor was the second of Edward's children to die. In 1871, his youngest son, Alexander John, had died just 24 hours after being born. Edward had insisted on placing Alexander John in a coffin personally with "the tears rolling down his cheeks".<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On his way to Denmark through Belgium on 4 April 1900, Edward was the victim of an attempted assassination when fifteen-year-old <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Sipido" title="Jean-Baptiste Sipido">Jean-Baptiste Sipido</a> shot at him in protest over the <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Second Boer War</a>. Sipido, though obviously guilty, was acquitted by a Belgian court because he was underage.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The perceived laxity of the Belgian authorities, combined with British disgust at Belgian atrocities in the <a href="/wiki/Congo_Free_State" title="Congo Free State">Congo</a>, worsened the already poor relations between the United Kingdom and the Continent. However, in the next ten years, Edward's affability and popularity, as well as his use of family connections, assisted Britain in building European alliances.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Accession">Accession</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_VII&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Accession">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>When Queen Victoria died on 22 January 1901, Edward became King of the United Kingdom, Emperor of India and, in an innovation, King of the <a href="/wiki/British_Dominions" class="mw-redirect" title="British Dominions">British Dominions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> He chose to reign under the name of Edward VII, instead of Albert Edward—the name his mother had intended for him to use<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup>—declaring that he did not wish to "undervalue the name of Albert" and diminish the status of his father with whom the "name should stand alone".<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The numeral VII was occasionally omitted in Scotland, even by the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">national church</a>, in deference to protests that the previous Edwards were English kings who had "been excluded from Scotland by battle".<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/J._B._Priestley" title="J. B. Priestley">J. B. Priestley</a> recalled, "I was only a child when he succeeded Victoria in 1901, but I can testify to his extraordinary popularity. He was in fact the most popular king England had known since the earlier 1660s."<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_VII_(Puck_magazine).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Edward_VII_%28Puck_magazine%29.jpg/170px-Edward_VII_%28Puck_magazine%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Edward_VII_%28Puck_magazine%29.jpg/255px-Edward_VII_%28Puck_magazine%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Edward_VII_%28Puck_magazine%29.jpg/340px-Edward_VII_%28Puck_magazine%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="460" data-file-height="616" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_VII_(Puck_magazine).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Caricature in <i><a href="/wiki/Puck_(magazine)" title="Puck (magazine)">Puck</a></i> magazine, 1901</div></div></div>
<p>Edward donated his parents' house, <a href="/wiki/Osborne_House" title="Osborne House">Osborne</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Wight" title="Isle of Wight">Isle of Wight</a>, to the state and continued to live at Sandringham.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> He could afford to be magnanimous; his private secretary, Sir <a href="/wiki/Francis_Knollys,_1st_Viscount_Knollys" title="Francis Knollys, 1st Viscount Knollys">Francis Knollys</a>, claimed that he was the first heir to succeed to the throne in credit.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Edward's finances had been ably managed by Sir <a href="/wiki/Dighton_Probyn" title="Dighton Probyn">Dighton Probyn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Comptroller_of_the_Household" title="Comptroller of the Household">Comptroller of the Household</a>, and had benefited from advice from Edward's financier friends, some of whom were Jewish, such as <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Cassel" title="Ernest Cassel">Ernest Cassel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maurice_de_Hirsch" title="Maurice de Hirsch">Maurice de Hirsch</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Rothschild_family" title="Rothschild family">Rothschild family</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> At a time of widespread <a href="/wiki/Anti-Semitism" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-Semitism">anti-Semitism</a>, Edward attracted criticism for openly socialising with Jews.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_Edward_VII_and_Alexandra" title="Coronation of Edward VII and Alexandra">Edward's coronation</a> had originally been scheduled for 26 June 1902. However, two days before he was diagnosed with <a href="/wiki/Appendicitis" title="Appendicitis">appendicitis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lee102_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lee102-75">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The disease was generally not treated operatively. It carried a high mortality rate, but developments in <a href="/wiki/Anaesthesia" class="mw-redirect" title="Anaesthesia">anaesthesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antisepsis" class="mw-redirect" title="Antisepsis">antisepsis</a> in the preceding 50 years made life-saving surgery possible.<sup id="cite_ref-app_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-app-76">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sir_Frederick_Treves" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Frederick Treves">Sir Frederick Treves</a>, with the support of <a href="/wiki/Lord_Lister" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Lister">Lord Lister</a>, performed a then-radical operation of draining a pint of pus from the infected <a href="/wiki/Abscess" title="Abscess">abscess</a> through a small incision (through &#x200b;<span class="frac nowrap">4<span class="visualhide">&#160;</span><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span>-inch thickness of belly fat and abdomen wall); this outcome showed thankfully that the cause was not cancer.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> The next day, Edward was sitting up in bed, smoking a cigar.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Two weeks later, it was announced that he was out of danger. Treves was honoured with a baronetcy (which the King had arranged before the operation)<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> and appendix surgery entered the medical mainstream.<sup id="cite_ref-app_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-app-76">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Edward was crowned at <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a> on 9 August 1902 by the 80-year-old <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Temple" title="Frederick Temple">Frederick Temple</a>, who died only four months later.<sup id="cite_ref-Lee102_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lee102-75">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Edward refurbished the royal palaces, reintroduced the traditional ceremonies, such as the <a href="/wiki/State_Opening_of_Parliament" title="State Opening of Parliament">State Opening of Parliament</a>, that his mother had forgone, and founded new <a href="/wiki/Order_(honour)" class="mw-redirect" title="Order (honour)">honours</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Merit" title="Order of Merit">Order of Merit</a>, to recognise contributions to the arts and sciences.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> In 1902, the Shah of Persia, <a href="/wiki/Mozzafar-al-Din" class="mw-redirect" title="Mozzafar-al-Din">Mozzafar-al-Din</a>, visited England expecting to receive the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Garter" title="Order of the Garter">Order of the Garter</a>. The King refused to bestow the honour on the Shah because the order was meant to be in his personal gift and the Foreign Secretary, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Petty-Fitzmaurice,_5th_Marquess_of_Lansdowne" title="Henry Petty-Fitzmaurice, 5th Marquess of Lansdowne">Lord Lansdowne</a>, had promised it without his consent. He also objected to inducting a Muslim into a Christian <a href="/wiki/Order_of_chivalry" title="Order of chivalry">order of chivalry</a>. His refusal threatened to damage British attempts to gain influence in Persia,<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> but Edward resented his ministers' attempts to reduce his traditional powers.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Eventually, he relented and Britain sent a special embassy to the Shah with a full Order of the Garter the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id=".22Uncle_of_Europe.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="&quot;Uncle_of_Europe&quot;">"Uncle of Europe"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_VII&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: &quot;Uncle of Europe&quot;">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:EdwardVII_at_Balmoral.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/EdwardVII_at_Balmoral.jpg/220px-EdwardVII_at_Balmoral.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="181" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/EdwardVII_at_Balmoral.jpg/330px-EdwardVII_at_Balmoral.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/EdwardVII_at_Balmoral.jpg/440px-EdwardVII_at_Balmoral.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1256" data-file-height="1036" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:EdwardVII_at_Balmoral.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Edward VII relaxing at <a href="/wiki/Balmoral_Castle" title="Balmoral Castle">Balmoral Castle</a>, photographed by his wife, Alexandra</div></div></div>
<p>As king, Edward's main interests lay in the fields of foreign affairs and naval and military matters. Fluent in French and German, he reinvented royal diplomacy by numerous state visits across Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> He took annual holidays in <a href="/wiki/Biarritz" title="Biarritz">Biarritz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mari%C3%A1nsk%C3%A9_L%C3%A1zn%C4%9B" title="Mariánské Lázně">Marienbad</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-duke_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-duke-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> One of his most important foreign trips was an official visit to France in May 1903 as the guest of President <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Loubet" title="Émile Loubet">Émile Loubet</a>. Following a visit to <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_XIII" title="Pope Leo XIII">Pope Leo XIII</a> in Rome, this trip helped create the atmosphere for the Anglo-French <a href="/wiki/Entente_Cordiale" title="Entente Cordiale">Entente Cordiale</a>, an agreement delineating British and French colonies in North Africa, and ruling out any future war between the two countries. The Entente was negotiated in 1904 between the French foreign minister, <a href="/wiki/Th%C3%A9ophile_Delcass%C3%A9" title="Théophile Delcassé">Théophile Delcassé</a>, and the British foreign secretary, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Petty-Fitzmaurice,_5th_Marquess_of_Lansdowne" title="Henry Petty-Fitzmaurice, 5th Marquess of Lansdowne">Lord Lansdowne</a>. It marked the end of centuries of Anglo-French rivalry and Britain's <a href="/wiki/Splendid_isolation" title="Splendid isolation">splendid isolation</a> from Continental affairs, and attempted to counterbalance the growing dominance of the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> and its ally, <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austria-Hungary</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Edward was related to nearly every other European monarch, and came to be known as the "uncle of Europe".<sup id="cite_ref-royal_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-royal-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/German_Emperor_Wilhelm_II" class="mw-redirect" title="German Emperor Wilhelm II">German Emperor Wilhelm II</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Nicholas_II_of_Russia" class="mw-redirect" title="Emperor Nicholas II of Russia">Emperor Nicholas II of Russia</a> were his nephews; <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria_Eugenia_of_Spain" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen Victoria Eugenia of Spain">Queen Victoria Eugenia of Spain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crown_Princess_Margaret_of_Sweden" class="mw-redirect" title="Crown Princess Margaret of Sweden">Crown Princess Margaret of Sweden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crown_Princess_Marie_of_Romania" class="mw-redirect" title="Crown Princess Marie of Romania">Crown Princess Marie of Romania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crown_Princess_Sophia_of_Greece" class="mw-redirect" title="Crown Princess Sophia of Greece">Crown Princess Sophia of Greece</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alexandra_Feodorovna_(Alix_of_Hesse)" title="Alexandra Feodorovna (Alix of Hesse)">Empress Alexandra of Russia</a> were his nieces; <a href="/wiki/King_Haakon_VII_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="King Haakon VII of Norway">King Haakon VII of Norway</a> was both his nephew and his son-in-law; kings <a href="/wiki/Frederick_VIII_of_Denmark" title="Frederick VIII of Denmark">Frederick VIII of Denmark</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_I_of_Greece" title="George I of Greece">George I of Greece</a> were his brothers-in-law; kings <a href="/wiki/Albert_I_of_Belgium" title="Albert I of Belgium">Albert I of Belgium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_of_Bulgaria" class="mw-redirect" title="Ferdinand of Bulgaria">Ferdinand of Bulgaria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_Portugal" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles I of Portugal">Charles I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manuel_II_of_Portugal" title="Manuel II of Portugal">Manuel II of Portugal</a> were his second cousins. Edward doted on his grandchildren, and indulged them, to the consternation of their governesses.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> However, there was one relation whom Edward did not like: Wilhelm II. His difficult relationship with his nephew exacerbated the tensions between Germany and Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 1908, during Edward's annual stay at Biarritz, he accepted the resignation of British Prime Minister Sir <a href="/wiki/Henry_Campbell-Bannerman" title="Henry Campbell-Bannerman">Henry Campbell-Bannerman</a>. In a break with precedent, Edward asked Campbell-Bannerman's successor, <a href="/wiki/H._H._Asquith" title="H. H. Asquith">H. H. Asquith</a>, to travel to Biarritz to <a href="/wiki/Kiss_hands" class="mw-redirect" title="Kiss hands">kiss hands</a>. Asquith complied, but the press criticised the action of the King in appointing a prime minister on foreign soil instead of returning to Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> In June 1908, Edward became the first reigning British monarch to visit the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a>, despite refusing to visit in 1906, when Anglo-Russian relations were strained in the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Japanese_War" title="Russo-Japanese War">Russo-Japanese War</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Dogger_Bank_incident" title="Dogger Bank incident">Dogger Bank incident</a>, and the Tsar's dissolution of the <a href="/wiki/Duma" title="Duma">Duma</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> The previous month, he visited the Scandinavian countries, becoming the first British monarch to visit Sweden.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_opinions">Political opinions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_VII&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Political opinions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Edward_VII_Vanity_Fair_19_June_1902.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/King_Edward_VII_Vanity_Fair_19_June_1902.jpg/220px-King_Edward_VII_Vanity_Fair_19_June_1902.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="358" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/38/King_Edward_VII_Vanity_Fair_19_June_1902.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="290" data-file-height="472" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Edward_VII_Vanity_Fair_19_June_1902.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Edward depicted in naval uniform by <i><a href="/wiki/Vanity_Fair_(UK_magazine)" title="Vanity Fair (UK magazine)">Vanity Fair</a></i> magazine, 1902</div></div></div>
<p>While Prince of Wales, Edward had to be dissuaded from breaking with constitutional precedent by openly voting for <a href="/wiki/W._E._Gladstone" class="mw-redirect" title="W. E. Gladstone">W. E. Gladstone</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1884" title="Representation of the People Act 1884">Representation of the People Bill (1884)</a> in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> On other matters he was less progressive: he did not, for example, favour giving <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage" title="Women&#39;s suffrage">votes to women</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> although he did suggest that the social reformer <a href="/wiki/Octavia_Hill" title="Octavia Hill">Octavia Hill</a> serve on the <a href="/wiki/Commission_for_Working_Class_Housing" class="mw-redirect" title="Commission for Working Class Housing">Commission for Working Class Housing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> He was also opposed to <a href="/wiki/Irish_Home_Rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Home Rule">Irish Home Rule</a>, instead preferring a form of <a href="/wiki/Dual_monarchy" title="Dual monarchy">dual monarchy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As Prince of Wales, Edward had come to enjoy warm and mutually respectful relations with Gladstone, whom his mother detested.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> But the statesman's son, <a href="/wiki/Home_Secretary" title="Home Secretary">Home Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Gladstone" class="mw-redirect" title="Herbert Gladstone">Herbert Gladstone</a>, angered the King by planning to permit Roman Catholic priests in vestments to carry the Host through the streets of London, and by appointing two ladies, <a href="/wiki/Lady_Frances_Balfour" class="mw-redirect" title="Lady Frances Balfour">Lady Frances Balfour</a> and Mrs <a href="/wiki/Harold_Tennant" title="Harold Tennant">H. J. Tennant</a>, to serve on a Royal Commission on reforming divorce law—Edward thought divorce could not be discussed with "delicacy or even decency" before ladies. Edward's biographer <a href="/wiki/Philip_Magnus-Allcroft" title="Philip Magnus-Allcroft">Philip Magnus</a> suggests that Gladstone may have become a whipping-boy for the King's general irritation with the Liberal government. Gladstone was sacked in the reshuffle the following year and the King agreed, with some reluctance, to appoint him <a href="/wiki/Governor-General_of_South_Africa" title="Governor-General of South Africa">Governor-General of South Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Edward involved himself heavily in discussions over army reform, the need for which had become apparent with the failings of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Second Boer War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> He supported the redesign of army command, the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Territorial_Force" title="Territorial Force">Territorial Force</a>, and the decision to provide an <a href="/wiki/British_Expeditionary_Force_(World_War_I)" title="British Expeditionary Force (World War I)">Expeditionary Force</a> supporting France in the event of war with Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Reform of the Royal Navy was also suggested, partly due to the ever-increasing Naval Estimates, and because of the emergence of the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_German_Navy" title="Imperial German Navy">Imperial German Navy</a> as a new strategic threat.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Ultimately a dispute arose between Admiral <a href="/wiki/Lord_Charles_Beresford" title="Lord Charles Beresford">Lord Charles Beresford</a>, who favoured increased spending and a broad deployment, and the <a href="/wiki/First_Sea_Lord" title="First Sea Lord">First Sea Lord</a> Admiral Sir <a href="/wiki/John_Fisher,_1st_Baron_Fisher" title="John Fisher, 1st Baron Fisher">John Fisher</a>, who favoured efficiency savings, scrapping obsolete vessels, and a strategic realignment of the Royal Navy relying on torpedo craft for home defence backed by the new <a href="/wiki/Dreadnought" title="Dreadnought">dreadnoughts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The King lent support to Fisher, in part because he disliked Beresford, and eventually Beresford was dismissed. Beresford continued his campaign outside of the navy and Fisher ultimately announced his resignation in late 1909, although the bulk of his policies were retained.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The King was intimately involved in the appointment of Fisher's successor as the Fisher-Beresford feud had split the service, and the only truly qualified figure known to be outside of both camps was <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Wilson_(Royal_Navy_officer)" title="Arthur Wilson (Royal Navy officer)">Sir Arthur Wilson</a>, who had retired in 1907.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Wilson was reluctant to return to active duty, but Edward persuaded him to do so, and Wilson became First Sea Lord on 25 January 1910.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Edward was rarely interested in politics, although his views on some issues were notably liberal for the time. During his reign he said use of the word "<a href="/wiki/Nigger" title="Nigger">nigger</a>" was "disgraceful", despite it then being in common parlance.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> In 1904, during an Anglo-German summit in <a href="/wiki/Kiel" title="Kiel">Kiel</a> between Wilhelm II and Edward, Wilhelm with the Russo-Japanese War in mind started to go on about the "<a href="/wiki/Yellow_Peril" title="Yellow Peril">Yellow Peril</a>", which he called "the greatest peril menacing&#160;... Christendom and European civilisation. If the Russians went on giving ground, the yellow race would, in twenty years time, be in <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a> and <a href="/wiki/Poznan" class="mw-redirect" title="Poznan">Posen</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-macdonogh_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-macdonogh-104">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Wilhelm went on to attack his British guests for supporting Japan against Russia, suggesting that the British were committing "race treason". In response, Edward stated that he "could not see it. The Japanese were an intelligent, brave and chivalrous nation, quite as civilised as the Europeans, from whom they only differed by the pigmentation of their skin".<sup id="cite_ref-macdonogh_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-macdonogh-104">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Although Edward lived a life of luxury often far removed from that of the majority of his subjects, his personal charm with all levels of society and his strong condemnation of prejudice went some way to assuage republican and racial tensions building during his lifetime.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Constitutional_crisis">Constitutional crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_VII&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Constitutional crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Francis_Derwent_Wood_-_Edouard_VII.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Francis_Derwent_Wood_-_Edouard_VII.jpg/170px-Francis_Derwent_Wood_-_Edouard_VII.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="241" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Francis_Derwent_Wood_-_Edouard_VII.jpg/255px-Francis_Derwent_Wood_-_Edouard_VII.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Francis_Derwent_Wood_-_Edouard_VII.jpg/340px-Francis_Derwent_Wood_-_Edouard_VII.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1601" data-file-height="2266" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Francis_Derwent_Wood_-_Edouard_VII.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust by <a href="/wiki/Francis_Derwent_Wood" title="Francis Derwent Wood">Francis Derwent Wood</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_VII_Halfpenny.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Edward_VII_Halfpenny.jpg/170px-Edward_VII_Halfpenny.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Edward_VII_Halfpenny.jpg/255px-Edward_VII_Halfpenny.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Edward_VII_Halfpenny.jpg/340px-Edward_VII_Halfpenny.jpg 2x" data-file-width="398" data-file-height="396" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_VII_Halfpenny.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Profile of Edward VII on a <a href="/wiki/Halfpenny_(British_pre-decimal_coin)" title="Halfpenny (British pre-decimal coin)">halfpenny</a>, 1902</div></div></div>
<p>In the last year of his life, Edward became embroiled in a constitutional crisis when the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a> majority in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> refused to pass the "<a href="/wiki/People%27s_Budget" title="People&#39;s Budget">People's Budget</a>" proposed by the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal</a> government of Prime Minister Asquith. The crisis eventually led—after Edward's death—to the removal of the Lords' right to veto legislation.
</p><p>The King was displeased at Liberal attacks on the peers, which included a polemical speech by <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a> at <a href="/wiki/Limehouse" title="Limehouse">Limehouse</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Cabinet minister <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> publicly demanded a general election, for which Asquith apologised to the King's adviser <a href="/wiki/Francis_Knollys,_1st_Viscount_Knollys" title="Francis Knollys, 1st Viscount Knollys">Lord Knollys</a> and rebuked Churchill at a Cabinet meeting. Edward was so dispirited at the tone of class warfare—although Asquith told him that party rancour had been just as bad over the <a href="/wiki/First_Home_Rule_Bill" class="mw-redirect" title="First Home Rule Bill">First Home Rule Bill</a> in 1886—that he introduced his son to <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_War" title="Secretary of State for War">Secretary of State for War</a> <a href="/wiki/Richard_Haldane" class="mw-redirect" title="Richard Haldane">Richard Haldane</a> as "the last King of England".<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> After the King's horse <a href="/wiki/Minoru_(horse)" title="Minoru (horse)">Minoru</a> won the Derby on 26 July 1909, he returned to the racetrack the following day, and laughed when a man shouted: "Now, King. You've won the Derby. Go back home and dissolve this bloody Parliament!"<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In vain, the King urged Conservative leaders <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Balfour" title="Arthur Balfour">Arthur Balfour</a> and Lord Lansdowne to pass the Budget, which <a href="/wiki/Reginald_Brett,_2nd_Viscount_Esher" title="Reginald Brett, 2nd Viscount Esher">Lord Esher</a> had advised him was not unusual, as Queen Victoria had helped to broker agreements between the two Houses over <a href="/wiki/Irish_disestablishment" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish disestablishment">Irish disestablishment</a> in 1869 and the <a href="/wiki/Third_Reform_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Third Reform Act">Third Reform Act</a> in 1884.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> On Asquith's advice, however, he did not offer them an election (at which, to judge from recent by-elections, they were likely to gain seats) as a reward for doing so.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Finance Bill passed the Commons on 5 November 1909, but was rejected by the Lords on 30 November; they instead passed a resolution of Lord Lansdowne's stating that they were entitled to oppose the bill as it lacked an electoral mandate. The King was annoyed that his efforts to urge passage of the budget had become public knowledge<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> and had forbidden Knollys, who was an active Liberal peer, from voting for the budget, although Knollys had suggested that this would be a suitable gesture to indicate royal desire to see the Budget pass.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> In December 1909, a proposal to create peers (to give the Liberals a majority in the Lords) or give the prime minister the right to do so was considered "outrageous" by Knollys, who thought the King should abdicate rather than agree to it.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/January_1910_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="January 1910 United Kingdom general election">January 1910 election</a> was dominated by talk of removing the Lords' veto. During the election campaign Lloyd George talked of "guarantees" and Asquith of "safeguards" that would be necessary before forming another Liberal government, but the King informed Asquith that he would not be willing to contemplate creating peers until after a second general election.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_12-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Balfour refused to be drawn on whether or not he would be willing to form a Conservative government, but advised the King not to promise to create peers until he had seen the terms of any proposed constitutional change.<sup id="cite_ref-Heffer,_pp._286–288_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heffer,_pp._286–288-114">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> During the campaign the leading Conservative <a href="/wiki/Walter_Long,_1st_Viscount_Long" title="Walter Long, 1st Viscount Long">Walter Long</a> had asked Knollys for permission to state that the King did not favour Irish Home Rule, but Knollys refused on the grounds that it was not appropriate for the monarch's views to be known in public.<sup id="cite_ref-Magnus,_p._547_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magnus,_p._547-115">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The election resulted in a <a href="/wiki/Hung_parliament" title="Hung parliament">hung parliament</a>, with the Liberal government dependent on the support of the third largest party, the <a href="/wiki/Irish_nationalists" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish nationalists">Irish nationalists</a>. The King suggested a compromise whereby only 50 peers from each side would be allowed to vote, which would also redress the large Conservative majority in the Lords, but <a href="/wiki/Robert_Crewe-Milnes,_1st_Marquess_of_Crewe" title="Robert Crewe-Milnes, 1st Marquess of Crewe">Lord Crewe</a>, Liberal leader in the Lords, advised that this would reduce the Lords' independence as only peers who were loyal party supporters would be picked.<sup id="cite_ref-Magnus,_p._547_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magnus,_p._547-115">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Pressure to remove the Lords' veto now came from the Irish nationalist MPs, who wanted to remove the Lords' ability to block the introduction of Home Rule. They threatened to vote against the Budget unless they had their way (an attempt by Lloyd George to win their support by amending whiskey duties was abandoned as the Cabinet felt this would recast the Budget too much). Asquith now revealed that there were no "guarantees" for the creation of peers. The Cabinet considered resigning and leaving it up to Balfour to try to form a Conservative government.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>The King's <a href="/wiki/Speech_from_the_Throne" class="mw-redirect" title="Speech from the Throne">Speech from the Throne</a> on 21 February made reference to introducing measures restricting the Lords' power of veto to one of delay, but Asquith inserted a phrase "in the opinion of my advisers" so the King could be seen to be distancing himself from the planned legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> The Commons passed resolutions on 14 April that would form the basis for the <a href="/wiki/1911_Parliament_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="1911 Parliament Act">1911 Parliament Act</a>: to remove the power of the Lords to veto money bills, to replace their veto of other bills with a power to delay, and to reduce the term of Parliament from seven years to five (the King would have preferred four<sup id="cite_ref-Heffer,_pp._286–288_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heffer,_pp._286–288-114">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>). But in that debate Asquith hinted—to ensure the support of the nationalist MPs—that he would ask the King to break the deadlock "in that Parliament" (i.e. contrary to Edward's earlier stipulation that there be a second election). The Budget was passed by both Commons and Lords in April.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By April the Palace was having secret talks with Balfour and <a href="/wiki/Randall_Davidson" title="Randall Davidson">Randall Davidson</a>, Archbishop of Canterbury, who both advised that the Liberals did not have sufficient mandate to demand the creation of peers. The King thought the whole proposal "simply disgusting" and that the government was "in the hands of <a href="/wiki/John_Redmond" title="John Redmond">Redmond</a> &amp; Co". Lord Crewe announced publicly that the government's wish to create peers should be treated as formal "ministerial advice" (which, by convention, the monarch must obey) although Lord Esher argued that the monarch was entitled <i>in extremis</i> to dismiss the government rather than take their "advice".<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Esher's view has been called "obsolete and unhelpful".<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_VII&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Funeral_of_King_Edward_VII" title="Funeral of King Edward VII">Funeral of King Edward VII</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Edward_VII_on_his_deathbed_in_Buckingham_Palace_in_1910_Wellcome_V0042567.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/King_Edward_VII_on_his_deathbed_in_Buckingham_Palace_in_1910_Wellcome_V0042567.jpg/220px-King_Edward_VII_on_his_deathbed_in_Buckingham_Palace_in_1910_Wellcome_V0042567.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="135" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/King_Edward_VII_on_his_deathbed_in_Buckingham_Palace_in_1910_Wellcome_V0042567.jpg/330px-King_Edward_VII_on_his_deathbed_in_Buckingham_Palace_in_1910_Wellcome_V0042567.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/King_Edward_VII_on_his_deathbed_in_Buckingham_Palace_in_1910_Wellcome_V0042567.jpg/440px-King_Edward_VII_on_his_deathbed_in_Buckingham_Palace_in_1910_Wellcome_V0042567.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3598" data-file-height="2216" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Edward_VII_on_his_deathbed_in_Buckingham_Palace_in_1910_Wellcome_V0042567.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Drawing of Edward on his deathbed in Buckingham Palace by Sir Luke Fildes, 1910</div></div></div>
